type="application/ld+json">[{"@context":"https://schema.org","@type":"NewsArticle","headline":"What Megyn Kelly gets right — and wrong — about Conclave ","description":"Is the awards darling about the papacy as “disgusting” as conservative Catholics want you to believe?","datePublished":"2025-01-12T11:00:00+00:00","dateModified":"2025-01-12T11:00:00+00:00","url":"https://www.vox.com/culture/394583/conclave-megyn-kelly-backlash-catholicism-review","mainEntityOfPage":"https://www.vox.com/culture/394583/conclave-megyn-kelly-backlash-catholicism-review","publisher":{"@type":"Organization","name":"Vox","logo":{"@type":"ImageObject","url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2024/04/default-og-image.png?quality=90&strip=all&crop=0,0.13712291199202,100,99.725754176016","width":"1200","height":"628"}},"articleSection":"Culture","keywords":["Culture"],"author":[{"@type":"Person","name":"Alex Abad-Santos","url":"https://www.vox.com/authors/alex-abad-santos"}],"isAccessibleForFree":false,"image":[{"@type":"ImageObject","url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/01_4230_C_TP_00015_R.jpg?quality=90&strip=all&crop=10.2600625,0,79.479875,100","height":"3329","width":"6358"},{"@type":"ImageObject","url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/01_4230_C_TP_00015_R.jpg?quality=90&strip=all&crop=18.790625,0,62.41875,100","height":"3329","width":"4993"},{"@type":"ImageObject","url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/01_4230_C_TP_00015_R.jpg?quality=90&strip=all&crop=29.19375,0,41.6125,100","height":"3329","width":"3328"}],"speakable":{"@type":"SpeakableSpecification","xpath":["/html/head/title","/html/head/meta[@name='description']/@content"]},"articleBody":"Megyn Kelly is impressive when you consider that she’s been able to create an entire career just from being mad at stuff. She is like the Rumplestiltskin of irritation — turning cranky thoughts into gold — or a blond Andy Rooney with consequences. From the idea of Santa Claus being Black to fighting with Jane Fonda for the right to ask a woman about her plastic surgery, the archives are stuffed with the things that Kelly does not enjoy. (She did, however, like Real Housewife Luann de Lesseps’s Diana Ross blackface costume enough to defend it on air.)\nConclave, the Golden Globe-winning drama about the election of a new pope, just happens to be the latest thing Kelly hates.&nbsp;\n“Just made the huge mistake of watching the much-celebrated Conclave & it is the most disgusting anti-Catholic film I have seen in a long time. Shame on Ralph Fiennes, Stanley Tucci & John Lithgow for starring in it & shame on director Edward Berger (among others),” Kelly wrote on X, formerly known as Twitter, making sure to get the full names of the biggest actors in the movie.&nbsp;\nConclave has been one of the bigger indie hits of the holiday season, bringing in $69 million worldwide on a reported $20 million budget, winning best screenplay at the Golden Globes, and featuring prominently in Oscar discussion. But the rollicking thriller — which focuses on the machinations of Cardinals gathered in Rome to pick a new pope — is stirring up controversy with the group of people most likely to be interested in the inner workings of the church: Catholics, like Kelly. Or, at least, some Catholics.&nbsp;\nWhy Megyn Kelly is mad at Conclave&nbsp;\nThere is a lot to unpack about Conclave. From political infighting among an international coterie of bishops, to nosy clerical gossiping, to Isabella Rossellini as a nun with ulterior motives, to car bombs, the movie — based on an airport novel of the same name by Robert Harris — is arguably overwrought and overstuffed, if endlessly fun.&nbsp;\nHowever, it’s the “twist” ending that Kelly seems to have a real issue with (and more on this, with spoilers, in just a moment).&nbsp;And, as Kelly cited, the film is being recognized on the awards circuit and may be one of the Best Picture nominees come Oscar time.&nbsp;\n[Image: https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2024/10/spoilers_below.webp?quality=90&strip=all]\nHere’s Kelly’s full rant:\n> Spoiler: They make THE POPE INTERSEX! This is the big exciting twist at the end. I wish I had known so I wouldn’t have watched it. There are almost no redeeming characters in the movie — every cardinal is morally bankrupt/repulsive. The only exception of course is the intersex pope (who - surprise! - has female reproductive parts) & the cardinal who keeps her secret - bc of course that kind of Catholic secret-keeping must be lionized. I’m disgusted. What a thing to release to streaming just in time for Christmas. They would never do this to Muslims, but Christians/Catholics are always fair game to mock/belittle/smear.\nThough Kelly phrases her tweet in tabloid fashion and uses capitalization to drum up the alarm, Conclave is not exactly a traditional trans narrative, although it would be impossible not to read it as political.&nbsp;\nWhat Kelly is referring to when she writes that “they make THE POPE INTERSEX” is the final act of the movie, in which newly confirmed Pope Innocent, formerly known as Cardinal Benitez (Carlos Diehz), reveals to the film’s protagonist, Fiennes’s Cardinal Lawrence, that he was born intersex, but only discovered his female internal sexual organs as an adult. Benitez had — with assistance from the late prior Pope — considered a laparoscopic hysterectomy to remove his uterus and ovaries. The reveal is surprising and, ideologically, something of a curveball.&nbsp;\nIn her tweet, Kelly misgenders Benitez, who identifies as male in the movie.&nbsp;\nBenitez explains that his faith and respect to God helped him decide not to have the surgery that would affirm his masculinity: “I am what God made me. And perhaps it is my difference that will make me useful.”\n[Image: Carlos Diehz in Conclave. Diehz play Cardinal Benitez, who figures into a major twist at the end of the movie. https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/4230_FP_00337.jpg?quality=90&strip=all]\nThis is the point that’s angered Kelly: The Catholic Church does not allow women to hold higher positions of power, which the final twist reveals to be the ultimate concern of Conclave. Benitez’s intersex identity and positioning as one of the film’s morally good characters not only opens a conversation about tolerance but also questions the rules set in place against women in the Church and the Church’s governing of women’s bodies.  \nBeing born intersex is no sin, but as Benitez explains, despite his unaltered exterior appearance, “my chromosomes would commonly define me as being a woman.”&nbsp;\nHaving female anatomy disqualifies him not only from being pope, but from becoming a priest at all. If his fellow cardinals knew his secret, they would be very unlikely to agree that there’s value in Benitez’s particular difference. And if they were allowed to have social media, perhaps some of the conservative wing would even tweet in the same incensed fashion as Kelly.&nbsp;\nLawrence understands this better than anyone. As the dean of cardinals, it’s his duty to make sure that the next pope will be a good leader, someone who will symbolize the Catholic faith. He knows that if Benitez’s secret gets out, it would not only threaten his papacy, but that the resulting fight would likely be a detriment to Catholicism at large.&nbsp;\nYet, when he presses Benitez about who else knows and when he hears Benitez’s answer about his faith and relationship with God, Lawrence finds peace. Safe in the belief that the new pope was chosen by God, Lawrence confirms Benitez’s election.&nbsp;\nIs Conclave really anti-Catholic?\nAfter winning the Golden Globe for Best Screenplay this week, Conclave writer Peter Straughan was asked about Kelly’s tirade. He explained that he hadn’t read her critique in full, but was told that she deemed his movie anti-Catholic and disrespectful.\n“I don’t think the film is anti-Catholic. I was brought up Catholic. I was an altar boy,” Straughan told Variety.\nStraughan explained that he wrote a script that showed the struggle between the Catholic faith and the very powerful men in charge of sculpting what said faith is supposed to look like. Their aspirations for goodness, their ambition, and their inevitable fallibility are all parts of their humanity. These stewards are not perfect, but they’ve been ordained to spread a divine message.&nbsp;\n“I think the core message of Conclave is about the church always having to re-find its spiritual core, because it deals so much with power,” Straughan said. “That’s always been a careful, difficult balance. To me, that was a very central Catholic ideal that I was brought up with. I stand by it.”\nKelly bashed Straughan’s explanation on her site, writing: “I have a very high threshold for offense. It is really hard to offend me. I guess I didn’t feel deeply offended by this, but I was unsettled by what I saw. And I was pissed.”\n[Image: Believe it or not Stanley Tucci (right) plays a man named Cardinal Bellini. https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/03_4230_C_TP_00004_R.jpg?quality=90&strip=all]\nKelly isn’t the only Catholic who’s taken umbrage with the movie and or found its ending salacious. Prior to the movie’s release, it was already buried by some Catholic critics because of the original book. “Given what we know about Conclave from the fawning critics, the novel, and what people associated with the film have said, Conclave is more a piece of anti-Catholic propaganda than it is a work of art,” reads a report from a Catholic League critic who did not see the movie.&nbsp;&nbsp;The Catholic League is known for calling out and encouraging the boycott of movies — famously Dogma, The Golden Compass, and now Conclave — it deems anti-Catholic, as well as studios like Fox and Disney.\nYet the clash between Catholic ideals and the messier reality of the humans who make up the church that Straughan gets at isn’t a particularly new or novel idea.&nbsp;\nFor the last two decades, a massive sex abuse scandal and the administrative effort to cover it up has been the dominant narrative about the Catholic Church. In Pope Francis’s various apologies to survivors (who have criticized him for not taking more accountability), he has spoken about the need to examine the “atrocities” of those in power and mistakes the Church has made, as well as the Church’s responsibility to its victims.&nbsp;\nEssentially: In order to move forward, the Church has to acknowledge that, sometimes, the people given the most power by the Church have abused it. \nWhile Kelly isn’t entirely wrong about Conclave’s plot — over the course of the voting, various members of the clergy are revealed to be engaged in bribery, smear campaigns, and inappropriate sexual relationships, while others are simply ambitious or politically minded —&nbsp;Kelly’s conflation of depicting fictional cardinals’ corruption with “mocking/smearing/belittling” reflects a lack of media comprehension. (Setting aside the fact that there are a number of redeeming characters, including Cardinals Lawrence and Bellini, played by Stanley Tucci, who simply don’t share Kelly’s conservative values.)&nbsp;\nDepicting a cardinal as corrupt or interested in power isn’t automatically a smear. The reality that Catholic leadership is made up of human beings, with all their faults and wants, striving for something higher, is part of what makes it beautiful and, ideally, transcendent. Further, the film shows the conclave explicitly voting against the various “morally bankrupt” cardinals when they’re made aware of the transgressions — a sign that this governing body doesn’t stand for corruption.&nbsp;\nWhat Kelly seems to want is something resembling a Catholic version of a Marvel superhero flick, or propaganda where priests are unquestionably good. One could make the compelling argument that the latter mentality is part of how clerical sexual abuse became an insidious, systemic problem. Questioning the tenets of the faith is, as the movie demonstrates, the only way to keep that faith strong.&nbsp;\nMegyn Kelly and Conclave can both be pro-Catholic, even if they disagree&nbsp;\nThe divide between Kelly and Straughan, two people raised in Catholic homes, comes down to opposing views of what Catholicism is supposed to be.&nbsp;\nBack in March 2024, Kelly wrote about her own relationship with Catholicism, specifically her struggles during the process of annulling her first marriage. In her crisis of faith, she writes, she experimented with going to an Episcopalian mass, but disliked it immensely, stating that she’s drawn to rigidity: “I realized there is a reason I’m generally drawn more to the faith that with which I was raised, where there are these strict rules that sometimes feel weird and intrusive, but they resonate with me.”\nKelly wants rules and absolutism. Conclave touts the beauty of uncertainty and doubt.\nAs dramatic and campy as Conclave gets — at one point, conservative Cardinal Tedesco (Sergio Castellitto) hits his vape with true artistry — it always comes back to the idea that Catholicism is a constantly evolving and immensely personal, living thing. It’s about all these bishops, with their different backgrounds and ethnicities, different experiences and ideas about their religion, coming together and uniting because of a common faith. Their fidelity to God transcends boundaries that would normally keep everyone apart. Conclave sees the beauty in those differences and how Catholicism persists, as flawed as its stewards may be.&nbsp;\n“Our faith is a living thing precisely because it walks hand-in-hand with doubt,” Cardinal Lawrence says in his sermon addressing his fellow cardinals. “If there was only certainty, and if there was no doubt, there would be no mystery, and therefore no need for faith.”\nCatholicism, like any religion, is open for interpretation.&nbsp;\nFour men wrote the gospels, and each one focuses on different aspects of Jesus’s life. People reading (usually priests) or listening (usually laypeople) to the gospels walk away with their own interpretations of the symbolism and take it into their own lives. No one Catholic person’s idea of spiritual identity is identical to another’s. But each individual idea, especially when aired out on a sizable platform, like Kelly has and as Straughan has with Conclave, may and often tell us more about the person, their priorities, their values, and their hypocrisies more than it ultimately defines Catholicism itself.&nbsp;"},{"@context":"https://schema.org","@type":"BreadcrumbList","itemListElement":[{"@type":"ListItem","position":1,"name":"Culture","item":"https://www.vox.com/culture"}]}]</script><link class="duet--media--caption xptnl10"><figcaption class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup xkp0cg2 xptnl12">Ralph Fiennes stars as Cardinal Lawrence in <em>Conclave</em>, a movie that Megyn Kelly did not enjoy.</figcaption> <cite class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup xkp0cg2 xptnl11">Focus Features</cite></div></div></div></div><div class="_135mano5"><div class="_1jvzqea1 _1jvzqea0"><div class="_1j8rw6n0 _1j8rw6n1"><div class="_1j8rw6n2"><img alt="Alex Abad-Santos" data-chromatic="ignore" loading="lazy" width="36" height="36" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="_1j8rw6n3" style="color:transparent" srcSet="https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/author_profile_images/195900/alex.0.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=48 1x, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/author_profile_images/195900/alex.0.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=96 2x" src="https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/chorus/author_profile_images/195900/alex.0.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=96"/></div><div class="_1j8rw6n4"><a class="_1j8rw6n5" href="/authors/alex-abad-santos">Alex Abad-Santos</a> <span class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup _1j8rw6n6">is a senior correspondent who explains what society obsesses over, from Marvel and movies to fitness and skin care. He came to Vox in 2014. Prior to that, he worked at the Atlantic.</span></div></div></div></div></div><div id="zephr-anchor" class="_1agbrixy"><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Megyn Kelly is impressive when you consider that she’s been able to create an entire career just from being mad at stuff. She is like the Rumplestiltskin of irritation — turning cranky thoughts into gold — or a blond Andy Rooney with consequences. From the idea of <a href="https://www.politico.com/blogs/media/2013/12/megyn-kelly-jesus-and-santa-were-white-179491">Santa Claus being Black</a> to <a href="https://www.cnn.com/2018/01/22/entertainment/megyn-kelly-jane-fonda-feud/index.html">fighting with Jane Fonda</a> for the right to ask a woman about her plastic surgery, the archives are stuffed with the things that Kelly does not enjoy. (She did, however, like Real Housewife Luann de Lesseps’s Diana Ross blackface costume enough to <a href="https://www.cnn.com/2018/10/23/media/megyn-kelly-blackface/index.html">defend it on air.</a>)</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1"><em>Conclave</em>, the Golden Globe-winning drama about the election of a new pope, just happens to be the latest thing Kelly hates. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">“Just made the huge mistake of watching the much-celebrated <em>Conclave</em> & it is the most disgusting anti-Catholic film I have seen in a long time. Shame on Ralph Fiennes, Stanley Tucci & John Lithgow for starring in it & shame on director Edward Berger (among others),” <a href="https://x.com/megynkelly/status/1875776752292675973?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw%7Ctwcamp%5Etweetembed%7Ctwterm%5E1875776752292675973%7Ctwgr%5E57e70387d9362fdc7b6c54a3c9d12c85dcc2009b%7Ctwcon%5Es1_&ref_url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.megynkelly.com%2F2025%2F01%2F06%2Fmegyn-kelly-slams-conclave-movie-as-anti-catholic%2F">Kelly wrote on X</a>, formerly known as Twitter, making sure to get the full names of the biggest actors in the movie. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1"><em>Conclave</em> has been one of the bigger indie hits of the holiday season, bringing in <a href="https://www.boxofficemojo.com/release/rl3898179585/">$69 million</a> worldwide on a <a href="https://collider.com/conclave-global-box-office-20-million/">reported $20 million budget</a>, winning best screenplay at the Golden Globes, and featuring prominently in <a href="https://www.hollywoodreporter.com/movies/movie-features/conclave-director-edward-berger-ralph-fiennes-oscars-1236035051/">Oscar discussion</a>. But the rollicking thriller — which focuses on the machinations of Cardinals gathered in Rome to pick a new pope — is stirring up controversy with the group of people most likely to be interested in the inner workings of the church: Catholics, like Kelly. Or, at least, some Catholics. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="why-megyn-kelly-is-mad-at-conclave" class="_7ka5u1 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h2 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Why Megyn Kelly is mad at <em>Conclave</em> </h2></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">There is a lot to unpack about <em>Conclave</em>. From political infighting among an international coterie of bishops, to nosy clerical gossiping, to Isabella Rossellini as a nun with ulterior motives, to car bombs, the movie — based on an airport novel of the same name by Robert Harris — is arguably overwrought and overstuffed, if endlessly fun. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">However, it’s the “twist” ending that Kelly seems to have a real issue with (and more on this, with spoilers, in just a moment). And, as Kelly cited, the film is being recognized on the awards circuit and may be one of the Best Picture nominees come Oscar time. </p></div><div class="duet--article--block-placement _80slsf1 _80slsf0 duet--article--article-body-component"><div style="position:relative"><div class="_1agbrixi"><div class=""><div style="background-image:none" class="duet--media--content-warning licp6k0"><div https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2024/10/spoilers_below.webp?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1920 1920w, src="https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2024/10/spoilers_below.webp?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2400"/></a></div></div></div><div class="duet--media--caption xptnl10"></div></div></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Here’s <a href="https://x.com/megynkelly/status/1875776752292675973?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw%7Ctwcamp%5Etweetembed%7Ctwterm%5E1875776752292675973%7Ctwgr%5E57e70387d9362fdc7b6c54a3c9d12c85dcc2009b%7Ctwcon%5Es1_&ref_url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.megynkelly.com%2F2025%2F01%2F06%2Fmegyn-kelly-slams-conclave-movie-as-anti-catholic%2F">Kelly’s full rant</a>:</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><blockquote class="duet--article--blockquote _1c7w9tz0 xkp0cg9"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup _1c7w9tz8 xkp0cg1">Spoiler: They make THE POPE INTERSEX! This is the big exciting twist at the end. I wish I had known so I wouldn’t have watched it. There are almost no redeeming characters in the movie — every cardinal is morally bankrupt/repulsive. The only exception of course is the intersex pope (who - surprise! - has female reproductive parts) & the cardinal who keeps her secret - bc of course that kind of Catholic secret-keeping must be lionized. I’m disgusted. What a thing to release to streaming just in time for Christmas. They would never do this to Muslims, but Christians/Catholics are always fair game to mock/belittle/smear.</p></blockquote></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Though Kelly phrases her tweet in tabloid fashion and uses capitalization to drum up the alarm, <em>Conclave </em>is not exactly a traditional trans narrative, although it would be impossible not to read it as political. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">What Kelly is referring to when she writes that “they make THE POPE INTERSEX” is the final act of the movie, in which newly confirmed Pope Innocent, formerly known as Cardinal Benitez (Carlos Diehz), reveals to the film’s protagonist, Fiennes’s Cardinal Lawrence, that he was born intersex, but only discovered his female internal sexual organs as an adult. Benitez had — with assistance from the late prior Pope — considered a laparoscopic hysterectomy to remove his uterus and ovaries. The reveal is surprising and, ideologically, something of a curveball. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">In her tweet, Kelly misgenders Benitez, who identifies as male in the movie. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Benitez explains that his faith and respect to God helped him decide not to have the surgery that would affirm his masculinity: “I am what God made me. And perhaps it is my difference that will make me useful.”</p></div><div class="duet--article--block-placement _80slsf1 _80slsf0 duet--article--article-body-component"><div style="position:relative"><div class="_1agbrixi"><div class=""><div style="background-image:none" class="duet--media--content-warning licp6k0"><div class="duet--article--image-gallery-image _1j8uwx0" style="aspect-ratio:2.407705" id="dngyOmltYWdlOjM5NDU4Ng=="><a href="https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/4230_FP_00337.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0,0,100,100" data-pswp-height="2492" data-pswp-width="6000" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer"><img alt="Carlos Diehz in Conclave. https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/4230_FP_00337.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=640 640w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/4230_FP_00337.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=750 750w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/4230_FP_00337.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=828 828w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/4230_FP_00337.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1080 1080w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/4230_FP_00337.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1200 1200w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/4230_FP_00337.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1440 1440w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/4230_FP_00337.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1920 1920w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/4230_FP_00337.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2048 2048w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/4230_FP_00337.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2400 2400w" src="https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/4230_FP_00337.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2400"/></a></div></div></div><div class="duet--media--caption xptnl10"><figcaption class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup xkp0cg2 xptnl12">Carlos Diehz in <em>Conclave</em>. Diehz play Cardinal Benitez, who figures into a major twist at the end of the movie.</figcaption> <cite class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup xkp0cg2 xptnl11">Courtesy: Focus Features</cite></div></div></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">This is the point that’s angered Kelly: The Catholic Church does not allow women to hold higher positions of power, which the final twist reveals to be the ultimate concern of <em>Conclave</em>. Benitez’s intersex identity and positioning as one of the film’s morally good characters not only opens a conversation about tolerance but also questions the rules set in place against women in the Church and the Church’s governing of women’s bodies. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Being born intersex is no sin, but as Benitez explains, despite his unaltered exterior appearance, “my chromosomes would commonly define me as being a woman.” </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Having female anatomy disqualifies him not only from being pope, but from becoming a priest at all. If his fellow cardinals knew his secret, they would be very unlikely to agree that there’s value in Benitez’s particular difference. And if they were allowed to have social media, perhaps some of the conservative wing would even tweet in the same incensed fashion as Kelly. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Lawrence understands this better than anyone. As the dean of cardinals, it’s his duty to make sure that the next pope will be a good leader, someone who will symbolize the Catholic faith. He knows that if Benitez’s secret gets out, it would not only threaten his papacy, but that the resulting fight would likely be a detriment to Catholicism at large. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Yet, when he presses Benitez about who else knows and when he hears Benitez’s answer about his faith and relationship with God, Lawrence finds peace. Safe in the belief that the new pope was chosen by God, Lawrence confirms Benitez’s election. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="is-conclave-really-anti-catholic" class="_7ka5u1 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h2 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Is <em>Conclave</em> really anti-Catholic?</h2></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">After winning the <a href="/culture/393458/golden-globes-2025-winners-emilia-perez-netflix-shogun-brutalist">Golden Globe</a> for Best Screenplay this week, <em>Conclave</em> writer Peter Straughan was asked about Kelly’s tirade. He explained that he hadn’t read her critique in full, but was told that she deemed his movie anti-Catholic and disrespectful.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">“I don’t think the film is anti-Catholic. I was brought up Catholic. I was an altar boy,” Straughan told <a href="https://variety.com/2025/film/news/conclave-megyn-kelly-anti-catholic-writer-1236266290/">Variety</a>.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Straughan explained that he wrote a script that showed the struggle between the Catholic faith and the very powerful men in charge of sculpting what said faith is supposed to look like. Their aspirations for goodness, their ambition, and their inevitable fallibility are all parts of their humanity. These stewards are not perfect, but they’ve been ordained to spread a divine message. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">“I think the core message of <em>Conclave</em> is about the church always having to re-find its spiritual core, because it deals so much with power,” Straughan said. “That’s always been a careful, difficult balance. To me, that was a very central Catholic ideal that I was brought up with. I stand by it.”</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1"><a href="https://www.megynkelly.com/2025/01/06/megyn-kelly-slams-conclave-movie-as-anti-catholic/">Kelly bashed</a> Straughan’s explanation on her site, writing: “I have a very high threshold for offense. It is really hard to offend me. I guess I didn’t feel deeply offended by this, but I was unsettled by what I saw. And I was pissed.”</p></div><div class="duet--article--block-placement _80slsf1 _80slsf0 duet--article--article-body-component"><div style="position:relative"><div class="_1agbrixi"><div class=""><div style="background-image:none" class="duet--media--content-warning licp6k0"><div class="duet--article--image-gallery-image _1j8uwx0" style="aspect-ratio:2.403124" id="dngyOmltYWdlOjM5NDU4NQ=="><a href="https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/03_4230_C_TP_00004_R.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0,0,100,100" data-pswp-height="3329" data-pswp-width="8000" target="_blank" https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/03_4230_C_TP_00004_R.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=1920 1920w, https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/03_4230_C_TP_00004_R.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2400 2400w" src="https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/03_4230_C_TP_00004_R.jpg?quality=90&amp;strip=all&amp;crop=0%2C0%2C100%2C100&amp;w=2400"/></a></div></div></div><div class="duet--media--caption xptnl10"><figcaption class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup xkp0cg2 xptnl12">Believe it or not Stanley Tucci (right) plays a man named Cardinal Bellini.</figcaption> <cite class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup xkp0cg2 xptnl11">Courtesy: Focus Features</cite></div></div></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Kelly isn’t the <a href="https://angelusnews.com/arts-culture/conclave-movie-review/">only</a> <a href="https://catholicvote.org/priest-warns-not-watch-conclave-movie-mockery-of-faith/">Catholic</a> who’s taken <a href="https://www.missiodeicatholic.org/p/why-as-catholics-we-should-not-support">umbrage</a> with the movie and or found its ending salacious. Prior to the movie’s release, it was already <a href="https://www.catholicleague.org/new-catholic-film-conclave-to-debut/">buried</a> by some Catholic critics because of the original book. “Given what we know about <em>Conclave</em> from the fawning critics, the novel, and what people associated with the film have said, <em>Conclave</em> is more a piece of anti-Catholic propaganda than it is a work of art,” reads a report from a <a href="https://www.catholicleague.org/new-catholic-film-conclave-to-debut/">Catholic League critic</a> who did not see the movie. The Catholic League is known for calling out and encouraging the boycott of movies — famously <em><a href="https://www.catholicleague.org/wp-content/uploads/1999/08/Dogma.pdf">Dogma</a></em>, <em><a href="https://www.catholicleague.org/the-golden-compass/">The Golden Compass</a>, </em>and now <em>Conclave</em> — it deems anti-Catholic, as well as studios like <a href="https://www.catholicleague.org/boycott-20th-century-fox/">Fox</a> and <a href="https://www.catholicleague.org/catholic-league-calls-for-boycott-of-disney/">Disney</a>.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Yet the clash between Catholic ideals and the messier reality of the humans who make up the church that Straughan gets at isn’t a particularly new or novel idea. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">For the last two decades, a <a href="/2018/8/20/17759694/pope-francis-catholic-sex-abuse-scandal-pennsylvania-report-apologizes">massive sex abuse scanda</a>l and the administrative effort to cover it up has been the dominant narrative about the Catholic Church. In Pope Francis’s various <a href="https://www.ncronline.org/news/justice/canada-pope-francis-apologizes-evil-sex-abuse-vows-never-again">apologies</a> to <a href="https://www.sltrib.com/religion/2024/10/01/pope-francis-leads-church-asking/">survivors</a> (who have <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2024/09/27/world/europe/pope-francis-belgium-sex-abuse.html">criticized</a> him for not taking more <a href="https://www.npr.org/2022/07/25/1113498723/pope-francis-apology-canada-residential-schools-indigenous-children">accountability</a>), he has spoken about the need to examine the “<a href="https://www.vaticannews.va/en/pope/news/2018-08/pope-francis-letter-people-of-god-sexual-abuse.html">atrocities</a>” of those in power and mistakes the Church has made, as well as the Church’s responsibility to its victims. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Essentially: In order to move forward, the Church has to acknowledge that, sometimes, the people given the most power by the Church have abused it. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">While Kelly isn’t entirely wrong about <em>Conclave</em>’s plot — over the course of the voting, various members of the clergy are revealed to be engaged in bribery, smear campaigns, and inappropriate sexual relationships, while others are simply ambitious or politically minded — Kelly’s conflation of depicting fictional cardinals’ corruption with “mocking/smearing/belittling” reflects a lack of media comprehension. (Setting aside the fact that there are a number of redeeming characters, including Cardinals Lawrence and Bellini, played by Stanley Tucci, who simply don’t share Kelly’s conservative values.) </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Depicting a cardinal as corrupt or interested in power isn’t automatically a smear. The reality that Catholic leadership is made up of human beings, with all their faults and wants, striving for something higher, is part of what makes it beautiful and, ideally, transcendent. Further, the film shows the conclave explicitly voting against the various “morally bankrupt” cardinals when they’re made aware of the transgressions — a sign that this governing body doesn’t stand for corruption. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">What Kelly seems to want is something resembling a Catholic version of a Marvel superhero flick, or propaganda where priests are unquestionably good. One could make the compelling argument that the latter mentality is part of how clerical sexual abuse became an insidious, systemic problem. Questioning the tenets of the faith is, as the movie demonstrates, the only way to keep that faith strong. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><div id="megyn-kelly-and-conclave-can-both-be-pro-catholic-even-if-they-disagree" class="_7ka5u1 _7ka5u0 duet--article--standard-heading xkp0cg1"><h2 class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup">Megyn Kelly and <em>Conclave</em> can both be pro-Catholic, even if they disagree </h2></div></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">The divide between Kelly and Straughan, two people raised in Catholic homes, comes down to opposing views of what Catholicism is supposed to be. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Back in March 2024, Kelly <a href="https://www.megynkelly.com/2024/03/29/megyn-opens-up-about-her-crisis-of-faith-and-going-through-the-catholic-annulment-process/">wrote</a> about her own relationship with Catholicism, specifically her struggles during the process of annulling her first marriage. In her crisis of faith, she writes, she experimented with going to an Episcopalian mass, but disliked it immensely, stating that she’s drawn to rigidity: “I realized there is a reason I’m generally drawn more to the faith that with which I was raised, where there are these strict rules that sometimes feel weird and intrusive, but they resonate with me.”</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Kelly wants rules and absolutism. <em>Conclave</em> touts the beauty of uncertainty and doubt.</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">As dramatic and campy as <em>Conclave</em> gets — at one point, conservative Cardinal Tedesco (Sergio Castellitto) hits his vape with true artistry — it always comes back to the idea that Catholicism is a constantly evolving and immensely personal, living thing. It’s about all these bishops, with their different backgrounds and ethnicities, different experiences and ideas about their religion, coming together and uniting because of a common faith. Their fidelity to God transcends boundaries that would normally keep everyone apart. <em>Conclave</em> sees the beauty in those differences and how Catholicism persists, as flawed as its stewards may be. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">“Our faith is a living thing precisely because it walks hand-in-hand with doubt,” Cardinal Lawrence says in his sermon addressing his fellow cardinals. “If there was only certainty, and if there was no doubt, there would be no mystery, and therefore no need for faith.”</p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Catholicism, like any religion, is open for interpretation. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1">Four men wrote the gospels, and each one focuses on different aspects of Jesus’s life. People reading (usually priests) or listening (usually laypeople) to the gospels walk away with their own interpretations of the symbolism and take it into their own lives. No one Catholic person’s idea of spiritual identity is identical to another’s. But each individual idea, especially when aired out on a sizable platform, like Kelly has and as Straughan has with <em>Conclave</em>, may and often tell us more about the person, their priorities, their values, and their hypocrisies more than it ultimately defines Catholicism itself. </p></div><div class="duet--article--article-body-component"><p class="duet--article--dangerously-set-cms-markup duet--article--standard-paragraph _1agbrixh lg8ac51 lg8ac50 xkp0cg1"></p></div><aside class="_1tzd3in0 _1agbrixx"><p class="_1tzd3in1">You’ve read <!-- -->1<!-- --> article<!-- --> in the last month</p><div class="_1tzd3in2"><div class="_1tzd3in3"><p class="_1tzd3in4">Here at Vox, we&#x27;re unwavering in our commitment to covering the issues that matter most to you — threats to democracy, immigration, reproductive rights, the environment, and the rising polarization across this country.</p><p class="_1tzd3in4">Our mission is to provide clear, accessible journalism that empowers you to stay informed and engaged in shaping our world. By becoming a<!-- --> <!-- -->Vox Member, you directly strengthen our ability to deliver in-depth, independent reporting that drives meaningful change.</p><p class="_1tzd3in4">We rely on readers like you — join us.</p></div><div class="_1tzd3in5"><img alt="Swati Sharma" loading="lazy" width="59" height="69" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent" "},"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","contents":{"html":"For the last two decades, a \u003ca href=\"https://www.vox.com/2018/8/20/17759694/pope-francis-catholic-sex-abuse-scandal-pennsylvania-report-apologizes\"\u003emassive sex abuse scanda\u003c/a\u003el and the administrative effort to cover it up has been the dominant narrative about the Catholic Church. In Pope Francis’s various \u003ca href=\"https://www.ncronline.org/news/justice/canada-pope-francis-apologizes-evil-sex-abuse-vows-never-again\"\u003eapologies\u003c/a\u003e to \u003ca href=\"https://www.sltrib.com/religion/2024/10/01/pope-francis-leads-church-asking/\"\u003esurvivors\u003c/a\u003e (who have \u003ca href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2024/09/27/world/europe/pope-francis-belgium-sex-abuse.html\"\u003ecriticized\u003c/a\u003e him for not taking more \u003ca href=\"https://www.npr.org/2022/07/25/1113498723/pope-francis-apology-canada-residential-schools-indigenous-children\"\u003eaccountability\u003c/a\u003e), he has spoken about the need to examine the “\u003ca href=\"https://www.vaticannews.va/en/pope/news/2018-08/pope-francis-letter-people-of-god-sexual-abuse.html\"\u003eatrocities\u003c/a\u003e” of those in power and mistakes the Church has made, as well as the Church’s responsibility to its victims. "},"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","contents":{"html":"Essentially: In order to move forward, the Church has to acknowledge that, sometimes, the people given the most power by the Church have abused it. "},"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","contents":{"html":"While Kelly isn’t entirely wrong about \u003cem\u003eConclave\u003c/em\u003e’s plot — over the course of the voting, various members of the clergy are revealed to be engaged in bribery, smear campaigns, and inappropriate sexual relationships, while others are simply ambitious or politically minded — Kelly’s conflation of depicting fictional cardinals’ corruption with “mocking/smearing/belittling” reflects a lack of media comprehension. (Setting aside the fact that there are a number of redeeming characters, including Cardinals Lawrence and Bellini, played by Stanley Tucci, who simply don’t share Kelly’s conservative values.) In her crisis of faith, she writes, she experimented with going to an Episcopalian mass, but disliked it immensely, stating that she’s drawn to rigidity: “I realized there is a reason I’m generally drawn more to the faith that with which I was raised, where there are these strict rules that sometimes feel weird and intrusive, but they resonate with me.”"},"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","contents":{"html":"Kelly wants rules and absolutism. \u003cem\u003eConclave\u003c/em\u003e touts the beauty of uncertainty and doubt."},"dropcap":false,"endmark":false,"lead":false},{"__isBlockType":"CoreParagraphBlockType","__typename":"CoreParagraphBlockType","contents":{"html":"As dramatic and campy as \u003cem\u003eConclave\u003c/em\u003e gets — at one point, conservative Cardinal Tedesco (Sergio Castellitto) hits his vape with true artistry — it always comes back to the idea that Catholicism is a constantly evolving and immensely personal, living thing. It’s about all these bishops, with their different backgrounds and ethnicities, different experiences and ideas about their religion, coming together and uniting because of a common faith. Their fidelity to God transcends boundaries that would normally keep everyone apart. \u003cem\u003eConclave\u003c/em\u003e sees the beauty in those differences and how Catholicism persists, as flawed as its stewards may be. No one Catholic person’s idea of spiritual identity is identical to another’s. But each individual idea, especially when aired out on a sizable platform, like Kelly has and as Straughan has with \u003cem\u003eConclave\u003c/em\u003e, may and often tell us more about the person, their priorities, their values, and their hypocrisies more than it ultimately defines Catholicism itself. (2024)","alt":"","originalHeight":3329,"originalWidth":8000,"thumbnails":{"social":{"url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/01_4230_C_TP_00015_R.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all\u0026crop=10.2600625,0,79.479875,100","mimeType":"imageJpg","normalizedRatio":1.91,"cropCoordinates":{"height":100,"width":79.47987499999999}}}}},"__isContentResourceInterface":"PostResourceType","dek":{"plaintext":"﻿Is the papal hit as “disgusting” as conservative Catholics want you to believe?","html":"﻿Is the papal hit as “disgusting” as conservative Catholics want you to believe?"},"robots":{"index":true,"follow":true},"canonicalUrlOverride":"","originalPublishedAt":"2025-01-12T11:00:00+00:00","publishedAt":"2025-01-12T11:00:00+00:00","__isFeedContentResourceInterface":"PostResourceType","seo":{"headline":"The Conclave twist that’s angered Megyn Kelly and conservative Catholics","description":"Is the awards darling about the papacy as “disgusting” as conservative Catholics want you to believe?"},"social":{"headline":"What Megyn Kelly gets right — and wrong — about Conclave ","description":"﻿Is the papal hit as “disgusting” as conservative Catholics want you to believe?","image":null},"promo":{"headline":"What Megyn Kelly gets right — and wrong — about Conclave "},"ledeMedia":{"__typename":"LedeMediaImageType","image":{"title":"CONCLAVE (2024)","alt":"","originalHeight":3329,"originalWidth":8000,"thumbnails":{"social":{"url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/01_4230_C_TP_00015_R.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all\u0026crop=10.2600625,0,79.479875,100","mimeType":"imageJpg","normalizedRatio":1.91,"cropCoordinates":{"height":100,"width":79.47987499999999}},"horizontal":{"url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/01_4230_C_TP_00015_R.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all\u0026crop=18.790625,0,62.41875,100","cropCoordinates":{"height":100,"width":62.41875}},"square":{"url":"https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/01_4230_C_TP_00015_R.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all\u0026crop=29.19375,0,41.6125,100","cropCoordinates":{"height":100,"width":41.6125}}},"id":"dngyOmltYWdlOjM5NDU4NA=="}},"updatedAt":"2025-01-10T23:35:00+00:00","createdAt":"2025-01-10T21:48:09+00:00","hasAffiliateLinks":false,"additionalBylines":"","status":"publish","wordCount":1991,"package":null,"streams":{"nodes":[]},"liveStatus":{"startAt":null,"status":false,"endAt":null},"isPaywallEligible":true,"seoArticleBody":"Megyn Kelly is impressive when you consider that she’s been able to create an entire career just from being mad at stuff. She is like the Rumplestiltskin of irritation — turning cranky thoughts into gold — or a blond Andy Rooney with consequences. From the idea of Santa Claus being Black to fighting with Jane Fonda for the right to ask a woman about her plastic surgery, the archives are stuffed with the things that Kelly does not enjoy. (She did, however, like Real Housewife Luann de Lesseps’s Diana Ross blackface costume enough to defend it on air.)\nConclave, the Golden Globe-winning drama about the election of a new pope, just happens to be the latest thing Kelly hates.\u0026nbsp;\n“Just made the huge mistake of watching the much-celebrated Conclave \u0026 it is the most disgusting anti-Catholic film I have seen in a long time. Shame on Ralph Fiennes, Stanley Tucci \u0026 John Lithgow for starring in it \u0026 shame on director Edward Berger (among others),” Kelly wrote on X, formerly known as Twitter, making sure to get the full names of the biggest actors in the movie.\u0026nbsp;\nConclave has been one of the bigger indie hits of the holiday season, bringing in $69 million worldwide on a reported $20 million budget, winning best screenplay at the Golden Globes, and featuring prominently in Oscar discussion. But the rollicking thriller — which focuses on the machinations of Cardinals gathered in Rome to pick a new pope — is stirring up controversy with the group of people most likely to be interested in the inner workings of the church: Catholics, like Kelly. Or, at least, some Catholics.\u0026nbsp;\nWhy Megyn Kelly is mad at Conclave\u0026nbsp;\nThere is a lot to unpack about Conclave. From political infighting among an international coterie of bishops, to nosy clerical gossiping, to Isabella Rossellini as a nun with ulterior motives, to car bombs, the movie — based on an airport novel of the same name by Robert Harris — is arguably overwrought and overstuffed, if endlessly fun.\u0026nbsp;\nHowever, it’s the “twist” ending that Kelly seems to have a real issue with (and more on this, with spoilers, in just a moment).\u0026nbsp;And, as Kelly cited, the film is being recognized on the awards circuit and may be one of the Best Picture nominees come Oscar time.\u0026nbsp;\n[Image: https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2024/10/spoilers_below.webp?quality=90\u0026strip=all]\nHere’s Kelly’s full rant:\n\u003e Spoiler: They make THE POPE INTERSEX! This is the big exciting twist at the end. I wish I had known so I wouldn’t have watched it. There are almost no redeeming characters in the movie — every cardinal is morally bankrupt/repulsive. The only exception of course is the intersex pope (who - surprise! - has female reproductive parts) \u0026 the cardinal who keeps her secret - bc of course that kind of Catholic secret-keeping must be lionized. I’m disgusted. What a thing to release to streaming just in time for Christmas. They would never do this to Muslims, but Christians/Catholics are always fair game to mock/belittle/smear.\nThough Kelly phrases her tweet in tabloid fashion and uses capitalization to drum up the alarm, Conclave is not exactly a traditional trans narrative, although it would be impossible not to read it as political.\u0026nbsp;\nWhat Kelly is referring to when she writes that “they make THE POPE INTERSEX” is the final act of the movie, in which newly confirmed Pope Innocent, formerly known as Cardinal Benitez (Carlos Diehz), reveals to the film’s protagonist, Fiennes’s Cardinal Lawrence, that he was born intersex, but only discovered his female internal sexual organs as an adult. Benitez had — with assistance from the late prior Pope — considered a laparoscopic hysterectomy to remove his uterus and ovaries. The reveal is surprising and, ideologically, something of a curveball.\u0026nbsp;\nIn her tweet, Kelly misgenders Benitez, who identifies as male in the movie.\u0026nbsp;\nBenitez explains that his faith and respect to God helped him decide not to have the surgery that would affirm his masculinity: “I am what God made me. And perhaps it is my difference that will make me useful.”\n[Image: Carlos Diehz in Conclave. Diehz play Cardinal Benitez, who figures into a major twist at the end of the movie. https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/4230_FP_00337.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all]\nThis is the point that’s angered Kelly: The Catholic Church does not allow women to hold higher positions of power, which the final twist reveals to be the ultimate concern of Conclave. Benitez’s intersex identity and positioning as one of the film’s morally good characters not only opens a conversation about tolerance but also questions the rules set in place against women in the Church and the Church’s governing of women’s bodies.  \nBeing born intersex is no sin, but as Benitez explains, despite his unaltered exterior appearance, “my chromosomes would commonly define me as being a woman.”\u0026nbsp;\nHaving female anatomy disqualifies him not only from being pope, but from becoming a priest at all. If his fellow cardinals knew his secret, they would be very unlikely to agree that there’s value in Benitez’s particular difference. And if they were allowed to have social media, perhaps some of the conservative wing would even tweet in the same incensed fashion as Kelly.\u0026nbsp;\nLawrence understands this better than anyone. As the dean of cardinals, it’s his duty to make sure that the next pope will be a good leader, someone who will symbolize the Catholic faith. He knows that if Benitez’s secret gets out, it would not only threaten his papacy, but that the resulting fight would likely be a detriment to Catholicism at large.\u0026nbsp;\nYet, when he presses Benitez about who else knows and when he hears Benitez’s answer about his faith and relationship with God, Lawrence finds peace. Safe in the belief that the new pope was chosen by God, Lawrence confirms Benitez’s election.\u0026nbsp;\nIs Conclave really anti-Catholic?\nAfter winning the Golden Globe for Best Screenplay this week, Conclave writer Peter Straughan was asked about Kelly’s tirade. He explained that he hadn’t read her critique in full, but was told that she deemed his movie anti-Catholic and disrespectful.\n“I don’t think the film is anti-Catholic. I was brought up Catholic. I was an altar boy,” Straughan told Variety.\nStraughan explained that he wrote a script that showed the struggle between the Catholic faith and the very powerful men in charge of sculpting what said faith is supposed to look like. Their aspirations for goodness, their ambition, and their inevitable fallibility are all parts of their humanity. These stewards are not perfect, but they’ve been ordained to spread a divine message.\u0026nbsp;\n“I think the core message of Conclave is about the church always having to re-find its spiritual core, because it deals so much with power,” Straughan said. “That’s always been a careful, difficult balance. To me, that was a very central Catholic ideal that I was brought up with. I stand by it.”\nKelly bashed Straughan’s explanation on her site, writing: “I have a very high threshold for offense. It is really hard to offend me. I guess I didn’t feel deeply offended by this, but I was unsettled by what I saw. And I was pissed.”\n[Image: Believe it or not Stanley Tucci (right) plays a man named Cardinal Bellini. https://platform.vox.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2025/01/03_4230_C_TP_00004_R.jpg?quality=90\u0026strip=all]\nKelly isn’t the only Catholic who’s taken umbrage with the movie and or found its ending salacious. Prior to the movie’s release, it was already buried by some Catholic critics because of the original book. “Given what we know about Conclave from the fawning critics, the novel, and what people associated with the film have said, Conclave is more a piece of anti-Catholic propaganda than it is a work of art,” reads a report from a Catholic League critic who did not see the movie.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;The Catholic League is known for calling out and encouraging the boycott of movies — famously Dogma, The Golden Compass, and now Conclave — it deems anti-Catholic, as well as studios like Fox and Disney.\nYet the clash between Catholic ideals and the messier reality of the humans who make up the church that Straughan gets at isn’t a particularly new or novel idea.\u0026nbsp;\nFor the last two decades, a massive sex abuse scandal and the administrative effort to cover it up has been the dominant narrative about the Catholic Church. In Pope Francis’s various apologies to survivors (who have criticized him for not taking more accountability), he has spoken about the need to examine the “atrocities” of those in power and mistakes the Church has made, as well as the Church’s responsibility to its victims.\u0026nbsp;\nEssentially: In order to move forward, the Church has to acknowledge that, sometimes, the people given the most power by the Church have abused it. \nWhile Kelly isn’t entirely wrong about Conclave’s plot — over the course of the voting, various members of the clergy are revealed to be engaged in bribery, smear campaigns, and inappropriate sexual relationships, while others are simply ambitious or politically minded —\u0026nbsp;Kelly’s conflation of depicting fictional cardinals’ corruption with “mocking/smearing/belittling” reflects a lack of media comprehension. (Setting aside the fact that there are a number of redeeming characters, including Cardinals Lawrence and Bellini, played by Stanley Tucci, who simply don’t share Kelly’s conservative values.)\u0026nbsp;\nDepicting a cardinal as corrupt or interested in power isn’t automatically a smear. The reality that Catholic leadership is made up of human beings, with all their faults and wants, striving for something higher, is part of what makes it beautiful and, ideally, transcendent. Further, the film shows the conclave explicitly voting against the various “morally bankrupt” cardinals when they’re made aware of the transgressions — a sign that this governing body doesn’t stand for corruption.\u0026nbsp;\nWhat Kelly seems to want is something resembling a Catholic version of a Marvel superhero flick, or propaganda where priests are unquestionably good. One could make the compelling argument that the latter mentality is part of how clerical sexual abuse became an insidious, systemic problem. Questioning the tenets of the faith is, as the movie demonstrates, the only way to keep that faith strong.\u0026nbsp;\nMegyn Kelly and Conclave can both be pro-Catholic, even if they disagree\u0026nbsp;\nThe divide between Kelly and Straughan, two people raised in Catholic homes, comes down to opposing views of what Catholicism is supposed to be.\u0026nbsp;\nBack in March 2024, Kelly wrote about her own relationship with Catholicism, specifically her struggles during the process of annulling her first marriage. In her crisis of faith, she writes, she experimented with going to an Episcopalian mass, but disliked it immensely, stating that she’s drawn to rigidity: “I realized there is a reason I’m generally drawn more to the faith that with which I was raised, where there are these strict rules that sometimes feel weird and intrusive, but they resonate with me.”\nKelly wants rules and absolutism. Conclave touts the beauty of uncertainty and doubt.\nAs dramatic and campy as Conclave gets — at one point, conservative Cardinal Tedesco (Sergio Castellitto) hits his vape with true artistry — it always comes back to the idea that Catholicism is a constantly evolving and immensely personal, living thing. It’s about all these bishops, with their different backgrounds and ethnicities, different experiences and ideas about their religion, coming together and uniting because of a common faith. Their fidelity to God transcends boundaries that would normally keep everyone apart. Conclave sees the beauty in those differences and how Catholicism persists, as flawed as its stewards may be.\u0026nbsp;\n“Our faith is a living thing precisely because it walks hand-in-hand with doubt,” Cardinal Lawrence says in his sermon addressing his fellow cardinals. “If there was only certainty, and if there was no doubt, there would be no mystery, and therefore no need for faith.”\nCatholicism, like any religion, is open for interpretation.\u0026nbsp;\nFour men wrote the gospels, and each one focuses on different aspects of Jesus’s life. People reading (usually priests) or listening (usually laypeople) to the gospels walk away with their own interpretations of the symbolism and take it into their own lives. No one Catholic person’s idea of spiritual identity is identical to another’s. But each individual idea, especially when aired out on a sizable platform, like Kelly has and as Straughan has with Conclave, may and often tell us more about the person, their priorities, their values, and their hypocrisies more than it ultimately defines Catholicism
